Dear Mr. Batchko: 
This geotechnical data report (GDR) presents the results of our field exploration 
and laboratory testing performed for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
(HHCTCP) from East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands in the Ewa area on the Island of Oahu, 
Hawaii. 
Geolabs understands the procurement of the construction contract for this project 
will be through a design-build delivery process. The information presented in this 
geotechnical data report is not intended for final project design and construction. A 
detailed, site-specific geotechnical investigation will need to be performed by the 
Design-Build Contractor’s Geotechnical Engineer-of-Record (Geotechnical Engineer) 
based on the contract requirements and at the actual project structure locations. 
Please note that the soil and core samples recovered during our field exploration 
(remaining after testing) will be stored for a period of one year from the date of this 
geotechnical data report. The samples will be discarded after that date unless 
arrangements are made for a longer storage period. Please contact our office for 
alternative sample storage requirements, if appropriate. 
If you have questions or need additional information, please contact our office. 
Very truly yours, 
GEOLABS, INC. 
       Robin M. Lim, P.E. 
          Vice President

SECTION 1.0 - PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) involves the 
construction and operation of a fixed-guideway transit system in the corridor between 
Kapolei and the University of Hawaii at Manoa with a branch to Waikiki on the Island of 
Oahu, Hawaii. This geotechnical data report presents the results of our field exploration 
and laboratory testing performed for the design-build segment of the HHCTCP 
extending from East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands in the Ewa area, corresponding to 
approximately Sta. 370+00 to Sta  750+00. The project location and general vicinity are 
shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
A summary of the field exploration program conducted for the afore-mentioned 
segment of the HHCTCP is presented below. Detailed information and discussions for 
each specific work item are provided in the respective appendices of this geotechnical 
data report.

SECTION 2.0 - SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program for the 
Conceptual Engineering phase was to obtain an overview of the surface and subsurface 
conditions along the design-build segment extending from East Kapolei to 
Pearl Highlands for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project to advance the 
project design and cost estimation. The emphasis of the field exploration and laboratory 
testing program was focused on the foundation design of the aerial fixed guideway 
structures. Our work was performed in general accordance with the scopes of services 
outlined in our fee proposals dated December 17, 2007 and September 26, 2008, 
the Professional Services Subcontract with PB Americas, Inc. entered into on 
December 4, 2007, and Amendment 1 to the Subcontract dated January 9, 2009. In 
order to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and laboratory testing 
program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available in-house geologic and soils information 
in the project vicinity. A list of the references used in the preparation of this 
geotechnical data report is presented in the reference section.  
2. 
Preparation and submittal of a field exploration work plan for the 
subsurface exploration. 
3. 
Preparation and submittal of an accident prevention plan with activity 
hazard analysis in support of our field exploration activities. 
4. 
Coordination with the State of Hawaii – Department of Transportation, 
Highways Division and the City and County of Honolulu - Department of 
Planning and Permitting to obtain the necessary excavation permits for the 
boring/SCPT locations within the public right-of-way. A copy of the typical 
City and State excavation permits are presented as Figures A-1 and A-2 in 
Appendix A. The balance of the excavation permits are on file with the 
Project Office. 
5. 
Coordination with D.R. Horton through PB Americas, Inc. to obtain the 
Right-of-Entry permit prior to our field exploration work at the boring 
locations located within their property. 
6. 
Coordination of utility toning and clearance of the boring/SCPT locations 
with various utility companies. 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 2 – SCOPE OF WORK 
W.O. 6000-10

Page 7 
Hawaii ● California 
7. 
Provision of police officers and safety devices for traffic control and/or lane 
closures at selected boring/SCPT locations during our field exploration 
program. 
8. 
Implementation of best management practices (BMPs) during our field 
exploration work within the highway and in other paved areas. 
9. 
Mobilization and demobilization of truck-mounted drill rigs, water trucks, 
and operators to and from the project site. 
10. 
Drilling and sampling of forty-two (42) borings extending to depths ranging 
from about 26 to 224 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface for 
a total of about 5,307 lineal feet of exploration. 
11. 
Backfilling of the drilled borings with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite 
pellets, bentonite chips, and Quikcrete upon completion of the soil and/or 
rock sampling program, with the exception of the groundwater monitoring 
point locations. 
12. 
Coordination of the field exploration and logging of the borings by our 
geologists. 
13. 
Performance of three (3) seismic piezocone penetration tests (SCPTu) at 
selected locations extending to depths ranging from about 55 to 82 feet 
below the existing pavement surface. 
14. 
Performance of shear wave velocity profiling using seismic piezocone 
penetration testing (SCPTu) equipment at eight (8) selected boring 
locations for seismic design considerations. 
15. 
Performance 
of 
refraction 
microtremor 
(ReMi) 
survey 
by 
our 
subconsultant, Zonge Geosciences, Inc., at five (5) selected locations. 
16. 
Performance of two hundred and sixty-seven (267) field point load tests on 
selected rock cores retrieved at eighteen (18) selected boring locations. 
17. 
Conversion of four (4) selected borings to groundwater monitoring points 
with the installation of a total of ten (10) vibrating wire piezometers with 
sand packs in a bentonite seal and traffic rated covers over the monitoring 
points. 
18. 
Monitoring of the groundwater levels in the groundwater monitoring points 
utilizing data loggers. 
19. 
Laboratory testing of selected soil and core samples retrieved from our 
field exploration as an aid in classifying the materials and evaluating their 
engineering properties.

SECTION 3.0 - SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu generally is composed of the weathered and dissected 
remnants of two extinct shield (domed) volcanoes – Waianae and Koolau. The older 
Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western third of Oahu’s landmass while the 
younger Koolau Volcano forms a major portion of the eastern two-thirds of Oahu’s 
landmass. Generally, it is believed that the Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes 
began erupting on the order of about 20 to 25 million years ago as deep ocean 
submarine volcanoes. Subaerial volcanic eruptions on the Island of Oahu ended 
approximately 2.2 to 3.5 million years ago in the Pleistocene Epoch. Furthermore, the 
Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano remained active; therefore 
the eastern flank of Waianae Volcano is partially overlapped and buried below the 
younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior portions of the Island of Oahu. 
In addition to the primary volcanic composition of the Island of Oahu, other 
secondary and generally more localized geologic earth materials comprise the lowland, 
coastal, and valley portions of the island. The secondary earth materials include various 
alluvial and colluvial deposits, oceanic and marine sediments, and submerged and 
emerged coralline reef materials. 
The proposed East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands transit corridor segment traverses 
along the lower distal flanks of the Waianae and Koolau Mountain Ranges (Ewa Plain). 
The Ewa Plain is a gently sloping land surface formed by the long-term accumulation of 
alluvial sediments consisting mainly of terrigenous clays and silts with some embedded 
cobbles and boulders derived from the weathering and decomposition of basaltic rock 
residing further upslope. Some pockets of marine sediments and coralline detritus 
materials were intercalated within the alluvial sediments as a result of past sea-level 
fluctuations.  
The Ewa Plain is dissected by various drainage gullies, which emanate in the 
upland areas and discharge into the Middle and East Lochs of Pearl Harbor. With the 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii ● California 
passage of time, some of the incised drainage channels meandered in course and were 
filled-in by more recent soft alluvial sediments. The deeper basaltic rock of the Waianae 
and Koolau Mountain Ranges is the primary aquifer and potable water source on the 
Island of Oahu. The subsurface geological conditions along the transit corridor reflect 
past glacio-eustatic sea-level fluctuations, which occurred most substantially during the 
Pleistocene Epoch. 
As mentioned previously, the subsurface geological conditions along the majority 
of the East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands transit corridor segment are typified by various 
terrigenous alluvial deposits with some embedded marine sediments (coralline detritus) 
overlying buried volcanic materials consisting of residual and saprolitic soils and 
weathered basaltic rock formation. The base of the stratigraphic section in this area is 
the volcanic materials associated with the Waianae and Koolau Basalt.  
The sedimentary wedge overlying the buried basaltic rock acts as a confining low 
permeable layer overlying the more permeable basal aquifer consisting of basaltic rock 
materials. On the Ewa Plain and vicinity, the sedimentary wedge of alluvial materials is 
also referred to as the “caprock” overlying the basal aquifer. The “caprock” keeps the 
basal aquifer under a confining water pressure; therefore, some artesian flow conditions 
may be encountered at the ground surface when the basal aquifer with sufficient head is 
pierced and a conduit is provided extending toward the ground surface. 
The following paragraphs provide a generalized description of the major 
processes that contributed to the geomorphic evolution of the Island of Oahu. 
The Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes formed during the late Pliocene and 
early Pleistocene Epochs by the sequential deposition of basaltic lava flows. As 
mentioned previously, the main shield-building activity ceased about 2.2 to 3.5 million 
years ago. Evidence from previous deep drilled wells indicates that the Island of Oahu 
has subsided by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of this early volcanic activity 
(Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii ● California 
During that period of slow island subsidence, coral-algal reefs grew and 
fossilized along the southern coast of the Island of Oahu. As a result, broad 
embayments formed that were bounded by fringing barrier reefs whose growth 
essentially kept pace with the slow rate of island subsidence. Thereafter, a series of 
shallow lagoons formed behind the barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine 
sediments accumulated within the lagoons (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (most recent Ice Age), many sea-level fluctuations 
occurred as a result of the waxing and waning of global continental glaciation. Generally 
speaking, as the great continental glaciers accumulated, the global level of the oceans 
fell because there was less water available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the 
glaciers receded (melted), the global level of the oceans rose because of the water 
influx. The landmass elevation of the Island of Oahu remained essentially stable during 
these sea-level changes; therefore, the fluctuations were eustatic in nature. The 
glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea that were either higher or lower 
relative to the present sea-level conditions. The following are some generalized 
examples of the geomorphic effects that resulted from the changes in sea level relative 
to the island landmass. 
The higher sea-level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and depositional 
fans composed of terrigenous sediment at the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea stands caused accumulation of coralline reef materials at the 
correspondingly higher elevations and the deposition of lagoonal and marine sediments 
in quite backwaters protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level stands caused streams to incise erosional valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef platforms. In addition, the subaerial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardening of the calcareous reef materials. Finally, 
renewed subaerial erosion (acting on the upper regions of the volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energy 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii ● California 
depositional environments, which is typified by a poorly sorted mix of alluvial materials 
with a greater range in particle sizes. 
During periods of no significant sea level change, continued stream flow 
extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the deposition of alluvium 
overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
Land development within the last 100 years along the proposed transit corridor 
has brought the area to its present form. Agricultural land use and other development 
projects have resulted in alterations to the natural terrain including the placement of 
earth fills, which may be of poor quality in terms of supporting large structural loads. 
3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed five main stratigraphic units within the East Kapolei to 
Pearl Highlands segment of the project. The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock 
materials of varying character and engineering properties. The five main stratigraphic 
units are listed below in the general order of geologic age, beginning with the oldest unit 
and ending with the youngest unit: 
Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
Fill (Rf) 
The following sections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the 
above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, past experience with similar 
materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at 
selected locations along the project segment. The approximate delineation of these 
stratigraphic units at the ground surface along the segment alignment is presented on 
the Geology Map, Plate 5. 
3.2.1 Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Koolau Basalt is the oldest geologic unit within the segment alignment extending 
from East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands. Generally, it consists of pahoehoe flows of 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii ● California 
basaltic lavas and may have a mantle of cobbles and boulders, or residual soils, 
overlying the top of the basalt rock formation. In addition, it is anticipated that 
various types of volcanic rock sub-components, such as clinker layers and lava 
tubes, also may be encountered within the basalt rock formation. 
Structurally, Koolau Basalt and its subunits are among the strongest of all the 
units encountered within the project alignment under consideration. Therefore, 
the basalt formation and its related residual soils and saprolites make excellent 
load contributing capacity units and/or bearing formations due to their high 
strength characteristics in an undisturbed state. However, its use for foundation 
support in some areas of the project segment is limited by the considerable 
depths required to reach the top of the basalt and the artesian groundwater 
conditions expected where the basalt is encountered at relatively shallow depths. 
The following subunits of the Koolau Basalt are used in this geotechnical data 
report: 
Residual Soil 
This subunit consists of soils produced from the in-situ chemical 
weathering of the basic rock originating from Koolau Basalt. These soils 
generally consist of stiff to very stiff, brownish red or reddish brown clayey 
silts and silty clays. The soils are usually “structureless” and may have a 
blocky texture. 
Saprolite 
Saprolite is essentially derived from the tropical weathering of basaltic 
formations, which may contain intrusive rock bodies of variable 
composition. Saprolite is a form of residual soil which exhibits a relict 
structure from the parent rock. Structural features, such as vesicles and 
joints, are visible in saprolite. This subunit consists predominantly of 
clayey silt and silty clay soils containing variations in color with varying 
degrees of mottling. 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii ● California 
Clinker 
Clinker is the fragmental basalt found on the top, and sometimes at the 
bottom, of a’a lava flows. Clinker generally is gravel to cobble-sized 
fragments of scoriaceous rock in a tightly packed but open-graded 
structure. 
Basalt Formation 
This subunit is used to designate the in-situ lava formation. This subunit 
occurs as red, brown, or gray, severely fractured to massive, medium hard 
to very hard rock formation. 
3.2.2 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of 
time by more recent deposits to become somewhat consolidated, and then 
subsequently uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic 
processes. This unit generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of 
terrigenous sediments, which have been transported by stream action from the 
upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channel are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
may resemble saprolite soil deposits but are clearly differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit high strength, which leads to a 
relatively high bearing or load carrying capacity with generally low settlement 
characteristics. Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell 
potential when subjected to fluctuations in moisture content. However, this is not 
considered a constraint for the type of structural systems proposed for this 
project. Structurally, the Older Alluvium materials are considered to be sufficiently 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ● California 
competent to support relatively large structural loads with low settlement 
characteristics when designed properly. 
3.2.3 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital 
debris deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of 
any coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly 
cemented coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt sized clastic 
fragments. Some of the coralline fragments were dissolved and the solution 
cavities filled with cemented and/or uncemented fine sands and silts. 
Due to the granular nature of the majority of the coral-algal deposits and the 
variable degree of cementation frequently present, this formation generally has 
high in-situ strength characteristics. Structurally, the coral-algal reef deposits are 
capable of supporting heavy foundation loads with generally low settlement 
characteristics when designed properly. The following subunits are used in this 
geotechnical data report: 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The 
soils generally consist of medium dense to dense sandy gravel and 
gravelly sands. Silts and clays are only present in minor or trace amounts. 
Detritus also may be weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong 
cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original 
growth positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to 
well cemented. 
3.2.4 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ● California 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated sediments, which 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. Recent 
alluvium generally is found within some stream channels and bordering some 
stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors 
and basin features surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in 
proximity to swamp and tidal flat environments. 
The soils assigned to this unit generally are considered poorly to very poorly 
suited for use as support for foundation loads, due to their low in-situ strength 
characteristics and the tendency for excessive settlement to occur in structures 
overlying soils of this unit. The following subunits are used in this geotechnical 
data report. 
Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with 
fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized 
by a moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile 
mollusk shells. 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ● California 
3.2.5 Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low lying areas which were filled during 
urbanization. The majority of these low lying areas is underlain at relatively 
shallow depths by recent alluvium and was originally marsh areas. A small part of 
the filled lands along the segment alignment are underlain by older alluvial soil 
deposits. In areas underlain by the recent alluvium, the fill materials generally 
consist of clayey silts and sandy silts with stiff to hard consistencies. The fills 
were intended only to support very light structures on shallow spread footings 
and, therefore, only form a thin crust over the soft alluvial soils. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to 
make any general statement regarding their engineering properties or 
construction in these areas. 
Groundwater in the fill areas generally is expected to occur as a basal water 
table near sea level. However, higher perched groundwater tables may be 
encountered. 
3.2.6 Groundwater 
The segment alignment is located within the Southern Oahu regional aquifer 
system, which is the largest basal groundwater area on the Island of Oahu. Koolau 
Basalt is the primary aquifer in the central and eastern parts of the area, whereas 
the Waianae Volcanics forms the main aquifer in the western part. Unconsolidated 
and consolidated sedimentary deposits form a thick confining unit (“caprock”) near 
the coast. 
Water levels in the Southern Oahu groundwater area generally range from about 
25 to 30 feet above sea level inland to about 15 to 20 feet above sea level near the 
shore where the water is under artesian pressure because it is confined by caprock. 
The caprock impedes the seaward movement of fresh groundwater. In the eastern 
part of the area, thick valley fill and underlying weathered rocks form partial barriers 
to groundwater flow. In the western part of the area, the weathered zone near the 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii ● California 
unconformity separating Koolau Basalt from the underlying Waianae Volcanics 
impedes the flow of water between the two volcanic rock aquifers. 
We encountered groundwater in the borings drilled along the project alignment at 
depths ranging from about 4 to 93 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface 
at the time of our field exploration. The groundwater levels measured generally 
correspond to about Elevations -26 to +44 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL). It should be 
noted that groundwater levels could fluctuate depending on seasonal precipitation, 
groundwater withdrawal and/or injection, and other factors. 
In addition, we wish to point out that we encountered artesian groundwater 
conditions in the Waipahu area of the alignment. Specifically, we encountered 
artesian groundwater conditions in Boring Nos. 106, 110, 217, 218, 219, and 220 
at various depths below the existing ground surface during the course of our 
drilling operations.  
It should be noted that the segment alignment is located within the Pearl Harbor 
artesian aquifer basins. As discussed previously, artesian groundwater occurs 
within the Koolau Basalt. The potentiometric head of this aquifer varies spatially, 
temporarily and in response to pumpage. Generally, there is high probability of 
encountering artesian groundwater at any location where the foundations 
encounter Koolau Basalt at about Elevation +20 feet MSL or lower.

SECTION 4.0 - GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the design-build segment of 
the HHCTCP generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work components. 
A brief description of each work component is presented below. Detailed discussions, 
findings, and test results of the various components are provided in the respective 
appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in 
the introduction to Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the 
methods and procedures. 
Typically, the soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch diameter 
continuous-flight solid-stem augers and continued with PQ-size wireline coring methods 
to advance the PQ size casing. Sampling of the soils was performed using the modified 
California sampler (ASTM D 3550) and the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split spoon 
sampler (ASTM D 1586). In the softer soils, relatively undisturbed samples were 
obtained by pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil 
formation (ASTM D 1587).  
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil 
formations, samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel also were obtained 
and retained in core boxes for visual observation. In some cases, the borehole was 
advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a rotary wash drilling method; therefore, there 
were no soil samples retrieved between the soil sampling points (soil sampling by the 
modified California sampler or the split spoon sampler for the Standard Penetration 
Test). The borings were advanced into the rock formations with the PQ-size wireline 
coring methods. The PQ size wireline coring methods produce rock core samples that 
are 3.35 inches (or 85 mm) in diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586. SPT sampling provides a measure of 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 20 
Hawaii ● California 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured 
as described in Appendix A. ASTM D 6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining 
Rock Quality Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ 
with core diameters of 36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally 
acceptable for measuring RQD as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do 
not cause excessive core breakage or poor recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm 
[2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are the optimal core sizes for measuring 
RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters provided the core diameter is 
clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in Appendix H for information 
and reference. 
During the field exploration program, our geologists performed pocket 
penetrometer tests on selected cohesive soil samples and point load test on selected 
rock specimens. Pocket penetrometer tests provide an indication of the unconfined 
compressive strength of a soil sample. Point load tests are index strength tests for rock 
materials. The pocket penetrometer test values are shown on the Logs of Borings in 
Appendix A, and the point load test results are included in Appendix E. 
4.2 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
Seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) was performed at three selected 
locations, using seismic piezocone equipment, also referred to as Seismic CPT 
(SCPTu). The test procedures are discussed in more detail in Appendix B. In brief, a 
steel cone connected to a friction sleeve is pushed hydraulically into the soil, and the tip 
resistance, sleeve friction, and piezometric pore pressures are measured, providing 
information that can be correlated to the soil type and behavior in a virtually continuous 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii ● California 
profile with depth. SCPTu testing can be performed only to the depths to which the cone 
tip can be hydraulically pushed with our truck-mounted drill rig set up. At the 
three locations tested, all of which were in areas of recent alluvium, the SCPTu was 
terminated at depths of about 55 to 82 feet below existing grade. 
The seismic cone tip contains a geophone sensor. At a succession of depths, the 
advancement of the cone tip was halted, a seismic pulse was generated at the ground 
surface by striking a loaded plank with a switched hammer, and the resulting seismic 
shear wave was detected at the geophone. The distance from the source and the wave 
arrival time allow for determination of the average shear wave velocity over a depth 
interval. The seismic piezocone penetration test records are presented in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at eight (8) of the completed 
boreholes, using seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) equipment. 
Procedures are discussed in more detail in Appendix C. In brief, the completed borehole 
was tremie-filled with coated bentonite pellets in order to provide a medium through 
which the seismic cone tip could be pushed. The sleeve and tip resistance through the 
bentonite-filled borehole are not meaningful because the cone is pushed through the 
bentonite backfill; however, the seismic wave travels from the ground surface through 
the surrounding soils, allowing measurement of the average seismic shear wave 
velocity in the same manner as described above for the SCPTu. The measured average 
seismic shear wave velocity logs are presented in Appendix C. 
4.4 
Geophysical Investigations 
Surface geophysical seismic refraction surveys using the Microtremor Array 
(SeisOptReMi) methods were performed by Zonge Geosciences, Inc. along five (5) 
profiles lines. The profile lines were focused primarily in areas of recent alluvium where 
the objective was to obtain estimates of the depth and lateral extent of soft soil areas. 
The methodology and results of the geophysical investigations are presented in 
Appendix D. 
GDR 03/27/2009
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 22 
Hawaii ● California 
4.5 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
Vibrating wire piezometers were installed at four (4) test boring locations to 
monitor piezometric water levels and their seasonal fluctuations. Two to three (2 to 3) 
piezometer tips were installed at varying depths at each boring location to measure 
groundwater gradients. Data was recorded with a datalogger from the time of 
installation (varying from April through August 2008) through the termination of reporting 
in March 2009. Details of the piezometer installation and piezometric level readings are 
included in Appendix F. 
4.6 
Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock samples to determine the 
physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included moisture 
content, unit weight, Atterberg limits, grain size distribution, unconfined compressive 
strength of soil and rock, unconsolidated-undrained (UU) triaxial compression, 
consolidated-undrained (CU) triaxial compression, direct shear, consolidation, 
moisture-density relation (Modified Proctor), California Bearing Ratio (CBR), corrosion 
potential tests (pH, resistivity, chloride content, and sulfate content), ring swell, 
expansion index, and organic content tests. The laboratory test results are provided in 
Appendix G. 
END OF GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM

SECTION 5.0 - LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical 
data report are based upon information obtained from the field exploration points. 
Variations of subsurface conditions between and beyond the exploration points may 
occur, and the nature and extent of these variations may not become evident until 
additional field investigation and/or construction is underway. 
The locations and elevations of the field exploration points (with the following 
exceptions) indicated in this report have been surveyed by R.M. Towill Corporation 
following the completion of our field exploration program. The locations of B-101A, 
B-200, B-202, and B-301 were based on the latitude and longitude coordinates obtained 
from our handheld GPS units while these boring elevations are based on interpolation 
between contours shown on the September 2008 Plan and Profile Drawings provided 
by PB Americas, Inc. The physical locations and elevations of the field exploration 
points should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of 
borings in this report were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times 
shown on the graphic representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of PB Americas, Inc. and 
their client, City and County of Honolulu, Department of Transportation Services, for 
specific application to the East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands segment of the Honolulu 
High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project in accordance with generally accepted 
geotechnical engineering principles and practices.  No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This geotechnical data report presents information on the general subsurface 
conditions for a follow-on design-build delivery contract. This geotechnical data report is 
limited in scope and is for the project design advancement and cost estimation

APPENDIX A 
Test Boring Exploration 
GDR 03/27/2009
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page A-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions along the segment extending from 
East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands of the HHCTCP by drilling and sampling 42 borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. B-101, B-101A, B-102 through B-108, B-110 through B-112, 
B-200 through B-224, B-226, and B-301 through B-304. The borings extended to depths 
ranging from about 26 to 224 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface using 
various truck-mounted drill rigs equipped with continuous-flight augers and rotary coring 
tools. The field exploration was conducted from March 12 through December 12, 2008. 
The boring locations are summarized in Tables A-1.1 and A-1.2, and are shown on the 
Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.4. 
Soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch ID continuous-flight 
solid-stem augers, and continued with PQ-size wireline coring method to advance PQ size 
casing.  After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the boreholes, the 
bored holes (with the exception of those being converted to groundwater monitoring 
points) were generally backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite pellets (below 
the groundwater level) and bentonite chips (above the groundwater level). The temporary 
casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets 
starting from the bottom and advancing upward.  The top 3 feet of the borehole was then 
grouted with Quikcrete concrete mix.  No additives were added to the drilling water during 
our drilling operations. 
Our geologists classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on 
a near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by 
testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D 2487, 
Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil 
Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as 
groundwater levels, artesian groundwater condition, drilling rate, etc. are presented on the 
Logs of Borings, Plates A-1 through A-45. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in 
general accordance with ASTM D 3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from 
the borings drilled in general accordance with ASTM D 1586, Standard Test Method for 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.  
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page A-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Relatively “undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were obtained by pushing a 
3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil formation in general 
accordance with ASTM D 1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled Tube Sampling of Soils 
for Geotechnical Purposes. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the soil sample. Results of the pocket penetrometer 
tests are summarized on Tables G-1.1 through G-1.29 and are presented on the Logs of 
Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system with 
double tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2113, Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation.  Core drilling is a rotary drilling 
method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the 
hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. Rock 
cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown 
on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.2.  The Rock Description System is based on the 
publication “Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock 
Masses” by International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977).  
Recovery (REC) may be used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the 
relative quality of rock masses, where appropriate. Recovery is defined as the actual 
length of material recovered from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. 
For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery 
would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run that is sound 
material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, 
mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is 
recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on 
the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the 
relative quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of 
Rocks and Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989).

Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain rock volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by

APPENDIX B 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
GDR 03/27/2009
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page B-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
Geolabs, Inc. conducted cone penetration testing using the seismic piezocone 
penetration testing equipment at three probe locations, designated as B-109(CPT), 
B-218(CPT), and B-220(CPT), as shown in the following table and on the Boring 
Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.4. 
SEISMIC PIEZOCONE TESTING LOCATIONS 
Boring No. 
Station No. 
Northing 
Easting 
Surface 
Elevation 
(feet MSL) 
B-109(CPT) 
657+00 
79070.86 
1639625.49 
+8.6 
B-218(CPT) 
644+95 
78844.89 
1638423.98 
+7.6 
B-220(CPT) 
666+58 
79442.22 
1640500.01 
+8.3 
The test was performed in general accordance with ASTM D 5778 – Standard 
Test Method for Performing Electronic Friction Cone and Piezocone Penetration Testing 
of Soils. The probe extended to depths ranging from about 55 to 82 feet below the 
existing pavement surface using seismic piezocone penetration testing equipment 
(SCPTU) before refusal conditions to our system were encountered. 
The seismic piezocone penetration test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton 
steel electronic subtraction cone (Serial Number DSG1063) manufactured by Vertek 
with an apex angle of 60 degrees and an area of 1.55 square inches (10 square 
centimeters) connected to a friction sleeve with an area of 23.25 square inches 
(150 square centimeters), into the ground at a rate of approximately 20 mm/second 
(+5 mm/second) using a Mobile B-90 truck-mounted drill rig. A catalog cut of the 10-ton 
electronic subtraction cone and the manufacturer calibration report are presented in 
Figures B-1 and B-2 for illustration/information purposes only. 
The tip and side friction resistance and pore pressure induced immediately 
behind the cone tip (designated as the U2 parameter) during penetration are measured 
by strain gauges within the instrument and are recorded versus the depth of penetration 
at about 2-inch (5-centimeter) intervals. When the material encountered was too hard for 
further penetration by the SCPTU equipment, which might cause potential equipment 
damage, penetration was halted and the cone was withdrawn. 
The cone carries a uniaxial horizontal geophone with an embedded inclinometer 
to detect the arrival of a shear wave propagated from the ground surface. The seismic 
measurements are made when the SCPTU has stopped and a shear wave is sent into the 
subsurface. A shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a loaded plank in 
opposite directions with a switched hammer at approximately 6-foot intervals. The 
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix B 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page B-2 
Hawaii ● California 
propagation time of the wave from the hammer blow to the cone was measured at each 
depth interval. The vector difference of these depths divided by the time difference 
provided the average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The field data collected were subsequently processed in the office using the 
computer software program – CPT Coneplot developed by Vertek. Logs of the seismic 
piezocone penetration tests are presented on Plates B-1.1, B-2.1, and B-3.1. In general, 
the plots include cone tip resistance, friction ratio (sleeve friction over tip resistance), pore 
pressure, local friction, shear wave velocity, soil behavior (based on the Rf zone), and 
interpreted soil description. Values of the tip resistance, friction ratio, and pore pressure 
may be correlated to soil strength and classification. The center of the friction sleeve is 
approximately 4 inches (10 cm) behind the cone tip. To calculate the friction ratio, the 
average friction resistance and tip resistance are compared at the same depth. This 
involves an offset of the friction resistance by the physical distance of 4 inches. The soil 
behavior is determined using the average tip resistance and the friction ratio over 
approximately 14 inches (35 centimeters) using Robertson’s 1986 soil classification chart 
as shown on Figure B-3. 
Pore pressure is presented in terms of equivalent height of water head (fresh 
water). The equilibrium water pressure was estimated based on depth below water 
surface at the test location and the density of fresh water.  
The shear wave velocities measured at the probe locations are presented on 
Plates B-1.2, B-2.2, and B-3.2. Digital records of the seismic piezocone penetration 
tests are on file in the Project Office.

Cones & Data Acquisition Systems
10 & 15 Ton Digital Cones for E4FCS
Temperature Compensated - The digital electronic subtraction 10 and 15 
ton cones have been developed to address the accuracy, sensitivity and dura-
bility problems inherent in other cone designs, with a resolution of 6.5 and 10 
Ibs. respectively. The unit consists of a single element strain gauge transducer, 
eliminating mechanical coupling effects, and with cone support electron-
ics packaged directly behind the transducer. Both the cone tip, local friction 
element and pore pressure element are ﬁ eld replaceable by the operator in 
approximately ﬁ ve minutes. The cones are conﬁ gured with one through ﬁ ve 
channel capacity with six channels from which to choose.
Cone Channels:
•  Tip
•  Local Friction
•  Pore Pressure
•  Temperature
•  Inclination
•  Seismic
•  Resistivity Module can be added
There are two possible locations for pore pressure: on the tip or directly be-
hind the tip.
The electronics have precision power supplies for the strain gauges, which 
eliminate the effects of cable resistance on the measurements. Subtraction is 
performed digitally by the CPU and calibration data for tip, local friction and 
pore pressure channels is stored in CPU non-volatile memory.  Digital cone 
calibration is capable of correcting for non-linearity and hysterisis in a way 
that analog circuitry cannot. Digital cones conform to all international stan-
dards.

Electrical Speciﬁ cations
- Power: +12 Volt at 200 mA (max)
- Connector: 10 pin LEMO waterproof connector
- Operating temperature range: 0oC to 60oC
- Storage temperature: -40oC to 70oC
- 4 channel, 24 bit A/D converter for measurement 
   of tip, friction, and pore pressure.  Minimum 
   noise free resolution of 1 part in 65000
- 10 channel, 12 bit A/D converter for measure
   ment of inclination, seismic, temperature and 
   spare channels
- 8 bit microcontroller with 8kb ﬂ ash memory for 
   cone identiﬁ cation and calibration data storage
- 32kb non-volatile ram for data storage
- RS-485 serial communication.  Cable lengths of 
  1000 ft possible.  CRC error checking to prevent 
  transmission of incorrect data
- Cones are interchangeable in the ﬁ eld.  Cones 
   can be calibrated in the ﬁ eld.  All calibration data 
   is contained in the cone
- Compatible with vision cone and fuel ﬂ uorescence  
   detector

- Tip area: 10 cm2  / 15 cm2

  TSF)
- Tip mechanical overload capacity: 300 %
- Accuracy: 0.2 %
- Temperature compensated
Sleeve
- Sleeve area: 225 cm2
- Sleeve capacity: 1 MPa
- Sleeve mechanical overload capacity: 200 %
- Accuracy:  0.2 %
- Temperature compensated
- Equal end area eliminates pore pressure 
   effects
Pore Pressure Transducer
- Pore pressure range: 3500 kPa (500 psi)
- Pore pressure mechanical overload pres
   sure: greater than 200 % 
- Accuracy: 0.5 %
- Temperature compensated
- Special cones available with three pressure 
   transducers for simultaneous measurement 
   of U1, U2, U3
Inclinometer
- Two channel inclinometer.  Pitch and roll 
   measurement provides information on cone 
   migration
- Uses accelerometers to measure inclina-
   tion.  No moving parts
- Range: 0 – 15 degrees
- Resolution: 0.1 degree
Seismic
- Uni or Biaxial geophone
- Capacity: +/- 2g
- True DC response
- Electronic offset eliminates effect of gravity.
- Digitized in cone and transmitted over se-
   rial port  No extra wires or analog mea-
   surement required

A P P E N D I X   C 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling of the subsurface materials was performed 
using seismic Cone Penetration Testing (SCPT) equipment. Shear wave velocity testing 
using seismic cone penetration testing equipment was performed at eight selected 
boring locations, designated as B-102(SW), B-107(SW), B-111(SW), B-208(SW), 
B-212(SW), B-218(SW), B-220(SW), and B-304(SW), as shown in the following table 
and on the Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.4. 
SEISMIC SHEAR WAVE VELOCITY TESTING LOCATIONS

+68.0 
The seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at approximately 6-foot 
intervals extending to depths ranging from about 118 to 152 feet below the existing 
ground/pavement surface. In order to conduct the shear wave velocity test in the 
borings, the test borings generally were advanced utilizing rotary coring methods 
(with the exception of B-102 and B-208) to the maximum depth of the borings. Logs of 
the materials encountered in the borings are presented in the Logs of Borings in 
Appendix A. After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the boreholes, 
the bored holes were backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite pellets. The 
temporary casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the 
bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and advancing upward. When the bentonite 
pellets are in contact with the groundwater in the borehole, the pellets started to hydrate 
slowly. As the bentonite pellets hydrate, they swell and soften. The probes were then 
pushed through the softened bentonite extending to depths ranging from about 118 to 
152 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface using the seismic piezocone 
penetration testing equipment (SCPTU). 
The seismic shear wave velocity test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton 
steel electronic subtraction cone into the bentonite backfilled borehole. The cone has an 
apex angle of 60 degrees and a projected surface area of 1.55 square inches 
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix C 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page C-2 
Hawaii ● California 
(10 square centimeters) equipped with an electronic depth sensor attached to the cone 
rod. The seismic cone carries a uniaxial horizontal geophone to detect the arrival of a 
shear wave generated and propagated from the ground surface. Seismic measurements 
are made when the SCPT has stopped and a shear wave is sent into the subsurface. A 
shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a loaded plank with a switched 
hammer. The propagation time of the wave from the hammer blow to the cone was 
measured at each discrete depth interval. The vector difference of these depths divided 
by the time difference for the shear wave to arrive at the various depths provided the 
average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The shear wave velocities measured and the average shear wave velocities 
calculated for the top 100 feet of the soil profile at the boring locations are presented on 
Plates C-1 through C-8 in Appendix C. The average shear wave velocities for the upper 
100 feet of the soil profile were calculated based on the Steps for Site Classification 
procedures described in Table C3.10.3.1-1 of the AASHTO LRFD Bridge Design 
Specifications (Interim 2008).

Refraction Microtremor Survey 
Surface geophysical seismic refraction surveys using the Microtremor Array 
(SeisOpt ReMi)

were

Inc. 
at 
five (5) selected locations, designated as Remi Lines 1 through 5, along the segment 
under consideration in this geotechnical data report. The survey results are used to 
provide an estimate on the depth to competent materials (basalt formation) and to 
estimate the depths of the soft soil deposits. In addition, the survey also provides an 
estimate of the seismic shear wave velocity of the subsurface materials.

GEOPHYSICAL INVESTIGATION REPORT 
2D Seismic Refraction MicroTremor Survey 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project

2 
Final Report 
Executive Summary 
Two-dimensional (2D) seismic refraction microtremor (ReMi) data were acquired, processed and 
interpreted for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project – East Kapolei to Pearl 
Highlands - Ewa, Oahu, Hawaii.  The primary objective was to determine the lateral and vertical 
extent of soft-soil conditions; and, a secondary objective was to determine the ability of passive-
wave seismic surveying to image the depth-to-bedrock.  Approximately 1.163-line miles (6,140 
line feet) of 2D ReMi data were acquired along 5 lines.  All five lines were positioned along the 
Farrington Highway. Seismic line locations were selected based on known and suspect soft soil 
conditions 
based 
on 
geotechnical 
drilling. 
Results 
indicate 
the 
seismic 
and 
geologic/geotechnical data could be integrated to yield useful information beneath the area 
investigated.  The seismic survey results provide good information regarding the presence of- 
and extent of- soft soils. Soft soils are defined by the IBC Seismic Design Code.  Additionally, 
the 2D seismic survey revealed the relief on the basaltic bedrock is considerable, where it can be 
encountered as shallow as ~45 feet to as deep as ~230 feet beneath the existing highway.  
Quality of the seismic data was very good, and a high degree of confidence is placed on the 
results presented herein.   
Introduction  
In August 2008, Zonge Geosciences, Inc. (Zonge) conducted a seismic survey for the Honolulu 
High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) under subcontract to Geolabs, Inc. 
(Geolabs), who is a Geotechnical sub-consultant to PB Americas on the project.  Figure 1 
outlines the general project area, which covers from East Kapolei on the west end to Pearl 
Highlands on the east end.  The seismic investigation was performed under the direction of 
Robin Lim, Vice President of Geolabs, as part of the on-going geotechnical investigation.  
Geolabs laid out the seismic line positions and lengths based on design needs and in conjunction 
with the PB Americas project team. 
Objectives of the seismic investigation were: 
1) Determine the lateral variability of the ‘soft’ soils deposits; and, 
2) Determine depth-to-bedrock. 
To meet these two objectives Zonge completed a two-dimensional (2D) refraction microtremor 
(ReMi) survey with intensive support from Geolabs. Geolabs support included traffic control, 
police escort, and daily logistical input from Mr. Rick Pu.  Site conditions along the Honolulu 
High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHTCP) were very similar throughout the geophysical 
survey area. Seismic data were collected along the Farrington Highway (Figure 1) where seismic 
lines were placed in traffic lanes (east or west bound), the median, or on a sidewalk depending 
on crew safety, day of the week, and accessibility.  The line layout portion of this survey 
required day-to-day attention by Geolabs personnel and Zonge is extremely appreciative of the 
support provided during the field investigation.  Additionally, Geolabs provided follow-up data, 
in the form of geologic boring logs and shear-wave velocity data that assisted with the 
interpretation.  As will be discussed in the results section of this report, Zonge was provided 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
3 
Final Report 
project stationing in the form of Track Alignment Plan and Profile drawings from Geolabs.  We 
understand that the stationing has changed occasionally, so the data (stationing and elevation) 
presented herein have been plotted according to the September 12, 2008 plan and profile 
drawings. 
The geologic setting for this project varies considerably along the survey area, but generally 
there are two layers – undifferentiated overburden soils overlying bedrock.  However, boring 
logs indicate the overlying soils are composed of a complex series of coarse-grained loose to 
dense sands and gravels (with cobbles) and range to fine-grained soft to very stiff clay.  In 
addition to this general soil sequence, interbedded layers of coralline, mudflow or clinker 
deposits were encountered within the overburden soils.  Bedrock is defined as basalt across the 
entire length of the seismic survey area; although, the basaltic bedrock has significant variability 
in the degree of weathering and/or fracture density.  Based on years of boring and geotechnical 
data collection in this area, the depth to bedrock was anticipated to vary considerably across the 
project site, with no consistent trend (i.e., a regional dip).  Topographic relief on the bedrock 
surface is primarily attributed to erosional features such as paleo-channels or relief caused at the 
time the basalt flow was deposited.  That is, there are ridges and valleys at the subsurface 
soil/bedrock contact.  The water table is generally shallow, but is also variable due to the 
complex fine-grained soils, course-grained (permeable) soils, and variable bedrock surface.  
Numerous borehole logs identifying lithology and geotechnical properties of the subsurface soils 
and bedrock were provided to Zonge for this investigation by Geolabs. 
The primary objective of this seismic investigation was to characterize the variability of the soil 
deposits.  The secondary objective was to image the soil / bedrock interface.  The upper 100 feet 
of subsurface was the primary depth of interest for this investigation.  Additionally, data from 
this geophysical investigation is available for other geotechnical considerations of the HHCTCP 
project. Resultant 2D seismic profile data are intended to assess conditions between and below 
existing borings. Therefore, an appropriate seismic method that could work under urban, noisy, 
heavy traffic conditions was required.  Additionally, the method needs to have little or no affect 
due to the presence of groundwater in the soil pore spaces or bedrock fractures.  The ReMi field 
method was selected because it satisfied both needs discussed above.  ReMi is a new, but rapidly 
becoming a standard and robust geophysical method for use in urban settings to derive the shear-
wave velocity to 100 feet (Vs100’) for IBC site classification (Louis, 2001).  It is a one-
dimensional (1D) ‘seismic sounding’ method that measures passive-surface wave dispersion to 
determine the shear-wave (S-wave) velocity distribution with depth below a surface array of 
standard refraction-type geophones.  The method is gaining acceptance for other applications 
beyond the IBC site classification.  Zonge has performed numerous 2D ReMi field programs, 
which is an extension of the more standard 1D Vs100’ field method, where imaging the soils / 
bedrock contact beneath rivers (e.g., saturated soil conditions) or in urban settings (e.g., noisy 
site conditions) prohibited use of typical refraction, reflection, or active surface-wave testing.  
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
4 
Final Report 
Data Acquisition 
Instrumentation:  Seismic ReMi data were acquired along the proposed HHCTCP site using a 
24-channel Seismic Source DAQ LinkII seismograph. This system represents a state of the art, 
24-bit floating-point seismograph that is connected to a field laptop via ethernet cable. Analog 
signals from vertically-oriented geophones are collected in the DAQ LinkII seismograph where 
it is anti-alias filtered, converted to a digital signal, transmitted to the laptop computer and then 
recorded on the hard drive. An array of 48 receivers (geophones) was placed on the ground along 
the survey line at 10-foot intervals. The receivers were all low-frequency 4.5-Hz vertical 
component, velocity-transducer geophones. A DAQ LinkII seismograph has a 24-channel 
capacity.  The 48-channel receiver array was coupled to a Geostuff roll-along box which 
determines which channels to activate (up to 24) per measurement.   
Each 1D ReMi sounding was acquired using a 24-channel active array and a backhoe for a 
‘passive’ energy source.  The backhoe was located between 100 and 200 feet off the end of the 
line.  Physical positioning of the backhoe was dictated by intersections and coned-off areas.  
Multiple records were acquired per active receiver array to generate a single sounding.  The 
active array (of 24 geophones) would increment up, or “roll-along”, by 4 receivers, thus 
providing a single 1D Vs100-type sounding every 40 feet along each line.  As will be described 
in the next section, ReMi sounding data are placed at the middle of the 230-foot receiver array.  
Because of this, each ReMi line for this project is a total of 230 feet longer than the 2D cross-
sections presented for each line. That is, for each line there were 12 geophones (115 feet) behind 
and ahead of the first and last 1D Vs100 sounding, respectively.  Table 1 identifies each line, the 
approximate stationing, line length, cross-section length, and its reference position.  Each line is 
different length, location and number of soundings.  This is primarily due to logistical issues for 
traffic control, but often limited by cultural restrictions such as bridge crossings.  No seismic 
method is capable of imaging the subsurface on elevated structures.  Lines 3 to 5 represent one 
continuous cross-section from approximate station 632+85 to 686+55. 
Coverage:  ReMi data were acquired along 5 survey lines. The general locations for each of the 
five lines are shown on Figure 2.  Table 1 lists the ReMi line numbers, the approximate 
stationing, associated line & cross-section lengths, as well as a reference position.  The line 
segments start at the west-most end of the survey area, near East Kapolei, and continue in a non-
continuous fashion to the Pearl Highlands at the east-most end, near the High School.  Location 
data in Table 1 indicate there is ‘nearly’ continuous line coverage for the survey area, except 
maybe a major intersection where safety issues prevented acquiring data across them.  But in 
most cases, because of the traffic & police control, lines were able to be pushed continuously 
through small intersections, turn-outs, turn-arounds, and retail entrances.  Lines were set up 
along either the median, in the right-most lane of traffic, or on the sidewalk, depending on the 
day of the week and anticipated vehicle traffic load.  Note that some of the line segments 
represent a continuous ‘sounding’ profile. For example, Lines 2, 3, 4 and 5 combine for nearly 
5,500 line feet of continuous cross-section.  The individual lines were acquired on separate dates 
that were most appropriate for the specific location.  Geolabs personnel assigned the place for 
data acquisition daily.  It took constant interaction between Zonge and Geolabs personnel to 
GDR 03/27/2009

confirm traffic control, police escort, backhoe operators, and highway officials for safe and 
reasonable working conditions to be achieved.  Thanks to this combined effort a successful and 
safe field effort was completed in closed traffic lanes.  Through the effort of the entire field crew, 
line coverage varied daily with a minimum line length of 960 feet, and a maximum line length of 
1,960 feet of data acquisition in a single field day.  Typically, about 1,500 to 1,600 of 2D data 
were acquired daily.

Geolabs provided Zonge with Google™ Earth images showing approximate line locations for 
use during the field work.  The images posted the ReMi lines and also the geotechnical boring 
locations for reference positioning only.  These images are useful for basic line locations with 
respect to intersections, buildings, and other physical landmarks (e.g., canals and drainage 
channels). 
ReMi Data:  The following section briefly describes the ReMi method.  Additional information 
is provided in Appendix A – Introduction to the ReMi Method. 
The ReMi method uses the same instrumentation and field layout as a standard seismic refraction 
survey. However, with this seismic method there are no predefined source points or any need for 
timed or ‘triggered’ seismic shots.  ReMi is classified as a passive surface-wave technique.  That 
is, the ReMi method uses ambient noise, or vibrational energy that exists at a site without the use 
of input energy from hammers or explosives like those used on refraction and reflection seismic 
surveys, or even multi-channel analysis of surface-wave (MASW) seismic surveys.  Ambient 
energy can be anything from foot traffic to vehicles, construction activities, tidal energy and 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
6 
Final Report 
microtremor earthquakes. All of these noise sources are present at the HHCTCP project site. 
However, it was clear for this project that the primary ambient noise was ‘along-the-line’ or 
parallel to the line, caused by heavy traffic.  This is not the best surface-wave energy to produce 
quality results, as documented by many surface wave experts (e.g., Miller, 2007).  Because the 
program used a single line where the receiver array and the (primary) ambient energy are 
parallel, that is the energy travels up and down the line, it was decided that an off-line high-
amplitude energy source would create more coherent surface-wave energy.  As a result of initial 
field tests conducted at the west end of the survey 
area (i.e., Line 1) it was deemed appropriate to use 
an active source through the use of a rubber-tired 
backhoe tamping the ground surface with the bucket 
using varying force.  The backhoe easily generated 
different frequency and amplitude surface waves. 
The inset photo shows the backhoe on the far end of 
an intersection producing surface wave energy into 
the receiver array.  The use of backhoes has been 
successfully applied on numerous ‘passive’ wave 
surveys.  Although it may appear to be an ‘active’ 
source, there is no timing required for the impacts or 
anything that governs of the scale, number or force 
of the impacts that generate the in-line energy.  The 
photo also shows the data control car (blue van), 
traffic cones, and police officer which were required 
to traverse intersections like the one shown. 
Either by active noise, ambient energy and other cultural disturbances, these small-strain 
vibrations causes surface wave energy to propagate up and down the line (in both directions).  
These are the seismic energy that is recorded / measured by the ReMi method.  Actual 
positioning of the active source energy (i.e., the backhoe) is not relevant for the ReMi data 
analysis.  For nearly all the sounding locations along the Farrington Highway at least ten (10), 
unfiltered, 30-second ambient and backhoe vibrational energy records were recorded on 24 of 
the 48 channel array using a 2 msec sample rate.  In order to roll-through busy intersections 
quickly only five (5) 30-second records were acquired.  These 5 records proved sufficient during 
data analysis to provide quality soundings beneath these areas.  Also, for quality control 
purposes, ReMi records were collected with and without the backhoe source.  This was done to 
compare the data quality (e.g., spectral amplitude) that would be achieved if the backhoe was not 
used.  Results from these comparison records will be discussed in the results section. 
The illustration below diagrams the ’roll-along’ nature of placing 48 receivers on the ground and 
recording seismic data on 24 active channels.  This set up allows the operator to quickly move up 
the active 24 channels and continue recording data while receivers are picked up behind the 
active array, and laid out ahead of the array.  The upper diagram (A) shows just the initial two 
cable set up with 48 receivers, while the lower diagram (B) identifies the sounding number and 
location(s) for a long profile.  This diagram illustrates the field set-up used for the HHCTCP 
investigation.  It is the lower diagram that clearly identifies that the first 115 feet and the last 115 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
7 
Final Report 
feet of seismic line that do not produce subsurface coverage (i.e., soundings) beneath those ends 
of the line(s). 
Data Processing 
1D ReMi: The ReMi method calculates the shear-wave velocity (Vs) of layers and the respective 
depths to interfaces beneath the refraction line as described by Louie (2001). The ReMi method 
is primarily used to calculate shear velocity versus depth at a point (1D) in order to differentiate 
between different soil and rock strata.  The method is a surface-wave technique that relates the 
Rayleigh-wave velocity to shear-wave velocity through an empirical relationship. 
Noise records (including backhoe hits) collected at this site were processed using the SeisOpt® 
ReMi™ software, © Optim LLC, 2005 (Louie, 2001). The ReMi technique is based on two 
fundamental concepts. The first is that seismic-refraction recording equipment can effectively 
record surface waves at frequencies as low as 2 Hz with the use of low-frequency geophones. 
The second idea is that a simple, two-dimensional slowness-frequency (p-f) transform of a 
microtremor record can separate Rayleigh surface waves from other seismic arrivals.  This 
separation allows recognition of the true phase velocity and discriminates against energy 
propagating in other modes without dispersion.  The advantages of ReMi from a seismic 
surveying point of view are several, including the following: It requires only standard refraction 
instrumentation; it requires no triggered source of wave energy; and, it will work best in 
seismically noisy urban settings. Traffic, construction, and wind responses of trees, buildings, 
and utility standards provide the surface wave energy (“source”) for the recordings. 
There are four processing steps to derive the 2D ReMi Vs profiles: 
Step 1: Create a velocity spectrum or slowness (p) versus frequency (f) curve (i.e., a p-f plot) 
from the noise records - The distinctive slope of dispersive surface waves is an advantage of the 
p-f analysis; body waves and airwaves cannot have such a slope. Even if most of the energy in a 
seismic record is in a phase other than Rayleigh waves, the p-f analysis will identify the 
dispersion of the surface waves.  
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
8 
Final Report 
Step 2: Pick the Rayleigh-wave dispersion - Picking is done along a lowest p-f envelope 
bounding the dispersed energy appearing on the p-f image. The picks thus discriminate against 
higher apparent velocities present when ‘noise’ impacts the linear array from off-line or out-of-
line directions. Picking a surface-wave dispersion curve along an envelope of the lowest phase 
velocities at each frequency has a further desirable effect. Because higher-mode Rayleigh waves 
have phase velocities above those of the fundamental mode, the refraction microtremor 
technique preferentially yields the fundamental-mode surface wave phase velocities.  
Step 3: Generate1D shear wave velocity models - The ReMi method interactively forward-
models the normal-mode dispersion data picked from the p-f images with a computer algorithm 
adapted from Saito (1979, 1988) by Yuehua Zeng(1992).  This algorithm produces results 
identical to those of the forward-modeling computer codes used by Iwata et al. (1998), and by 
Xia et al. (1999) within their inversion procedure.  The modeling iterates on phase velocity at 
each period (frequency).  The analysis approach and the propagation properties of surface waves 
allow velocity reversals (low Vs layers at depth) to be modeled successfully. 
Step 4: Generate 2D shear wave profiles – Once a series of 1D Vs soundings have been modeled, 
the data are entered into a contouring or smoothing algorithm that produces a 2D smooth-model 
from the 1D inverted models / soundings.  The 2D profile represents a cross-section of the 
subsurface with soundings spaced at 40 feet (for this project).  Initially an ‘unsmoothed’ profile 
is generated, which only plots the 1D sounding without any smoothing operation applied, then 
the smoothing is applied appropriately for the anticipated geologic setting.  A concerted effort is 
taken during Step 4 to ensure that any geologic or geotechnical data available are analyzed and 
integrated with the final processing step. Soil stiffness (e.g., relative density from blow counts) 
and rock competency (from drill core RQD), and depth that bedrock was encountered are 
important parameters for modeling (1D – Step 3) and generating 2D smooth ReMi profiles. 
Both the smoothed and unsmoothed model results are included in this report.  Results and 
interpretations discussed in the following section have been based on smooth model inversions 
and the resulting 2D cross-sections. Unsmoothed models have been included in Appendix B of 
this report.  The unsmoothed models are not referenced to stationing like the smooth model 
cross-sections (discussed in the next section). 
A few of these steps are diagrammatically shown on the following page, purely for illustrative 
purpose.  The figure is adopted from a Kansas Geologic Survey publication that refers to MASW 
type of surface-wave data, whereas this illustration has been modified to show noise records and 
associated passive-wave soundings (referred to as “X” positions). 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
9 
Final Report 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc.
