Results & Interpretations 
Results from the 2D ReMi analysis for Lines 1 through 5 are presented on Figures 3 through 7. 
These figures were generated in a manner that allows comparison from line to line; that is, each 
figure uses the same color (velocity) scale.  Lines 1 and 2 have the same vertical exaggeration, 
and Lines 3 through 5 are at the same vertical exaggeration.  Each figure presents up to 760 line 
feet of ReMi cross-section; therefore, the longer lines required additional pages and have been 
labeled A, B and C to accommodate the additional line length (see Table 1).  Table 1 presents the 
line lengths and respective stationing shown on Figures 3 through 7.  Depth of investigation was 
a minimum of 120 ft, but much more along Lines 3, 4 and 5, as shown in the figures.  The 
figures are 11 x 17 inch tabloid plates.  Stationing and elevation, as shown on each figure, were 
taken from the Track Alignment Plan and Profile drawings (dated September 12, 2008) provided 
to Zonge, and should be considered approximate.  Particularly the elevation data since the 
seismic line position may not have been at the position where the ‘existing ground’ elevations 
are shown on the plan and profile sheets.  
Where geotechnical borings were located along the 2D ReMi lines, their locations have been 
posted on the figures for integration of material property data with the ReMi shear-wave velocity 
results.  These boring data were provided by Geolabs.  Some of the boring locations are 
approximate as they had not been completed by the time the seismic investigation was 
concluded.  Two velocity contours have been placed on the cross-sections: 1) a 600 feet per 
second (ft/s) contour (shown in yellow) which represents an interpreted transition from soft / 
loose to stiff / dense soil deposits; and, 2) a 2,000 ft/s contour (shown in black) that is interpreted 
to best represent the sharp velocity gradient between soils and bedrock.  The basaltic bedrock 
surface, as described on Geolabs boring logs, shows a wide variation in the degree of weathering 
(e.g., slightly to extremely weathered), as well as a substantial difference in the fracture density 
(e.g., moderately to highly fractured).  Based on close inspection of the boring logs at the 
respective locations along the ReMi profiles, and based on experience, actual bedrock shear-
wave velocity likely ranges between about 1,800 and 2,400 ft/s at this project site. Fracturing 
significantly decreases the compressional- or shear-wave velocity of a rock formation.  
As described previously, and discussed in greater detail in Appendix A, the ReMi seismic 
technique is a seismic method that averages the subsurface bulk properties beneath a 24-channel, 
230-foot, receiver array.  Each sounding represents that bulk or average shear-wave velocity.  
The cross-sections shown on Figure 3 through 7 are the best representation of the lateral and 
vertical variation of soft soils, and the depth to- and geometry of- the basalt contact. These 
seismic data are the best interpretation of the material properties between, below and beyond 
geotechnical drill hole control.  The ReMi data indicate significant variation and relief on the 
bedrock surface, as anticipated, and there is considerable correlation between boring logs and 
bedrock depths as interpreted by 2D seismic profiling. 
“Soft soils” are defined, in this investigation, as undifferentiated soil deposits that have a shear-
wave velocity of less than 600 feet/second (ft/s). Soils with a shear-wave velocity of less than 
600 ft/s are classified as Class E - soft soils, according to the 2006 International Building Code. 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
11 
Final Report 
Soils with shear-wave velocities between 600 ft/s and 1,200 ft/s are classified as Class D - stiff 
soils.  Based on the yellow contour line (600 ft/s) shown on Figures 3 though 7, the thickness of 
soft soils varies considerably across the project area.  On the west end (i.e., Line 1) there are 
virtually no soft soils, but by the east end of Line 2 (~Station 632+45) the thickness of soft soil 
goes to nearly 60 feet bgs.  Beneath Line 3 the soft soils are about 20- to 30-feet thick, until 
about Station 641+00 (i.e., the middle of Line 3) where it thickens to greater than 80 feet.  At 
approximately station 647+00 the soft soils thin to about 50 feet thick (or less), and remain that 
way till about Station 666+00 beneath Line 4.  Beneath the east end of Line 4 and the west end 
of Line 5 the soft soils range from about 60 to 80 feet thick.  However, east of Station 671+00 
the soft soils (like the soil/bedrock interface) shallow considerably, and by the middle of Line 5 
(~Station 679+00) the interpreted 2D ReMi results do not show any soft soils extending to the 
east end of the project area (near the high school). 
In general, results from the west end of the project area (Lines 1 and 2) show the bedrock contact 
is relatively flat and nearly a constant depth at ~60 to 65 feet below ground surface (bgs).  A 
small hump in the interpreted bedrock surface near the middle of Line 2 (~Station 631+00) may 
be caused the presence of very dense cobbles and boulders as described on the boring log for B-
216; possibly a high-energy dense gravel deposit overlying bedrock.  East of B-217 along Line 3 
there is a definite deepening of the bedrock surface to greater than 120 feet at about Station 
646+50 on Line 3. This trend is confirmed further east along Line 4 at approximate Station 
650+50 feet near the boring B-110.  The correlation of ReMi shear-wave velocities with 
velocities measured by Geolabs in B-109(SW) is very good with respect to the soft soil contact 
at a depth of about 45 feet; and similarly, that B-110 (which terminated at ~177 feet bgs without 
encountering bedrock) supports the geophysical interpretation that bedrock is deep beneath this 
portion of the project area.  Along this east end of Line 4 the depth to bedrock determined by 2D 
ReMi imaging is the deepest for the area investigated (~230 feet).  It should be noted that this 
depth is at the absolute maximum resolvable depth with the field set-up and instrumentation 
used, and thereby we have slightly lower confidence in this very deep data than for depth ranges 
within the upper 100 to 150 feet.  However, the general geometry and approximate depth of this 
anomalously deep bedrock area are considered to be valid interpretations of the data.  As this 
long continuous cross-section continues toward Line 5, bedrock slowly rises to less than about 
100 feet deep, and continues to rise until the position of B-221.  Although the tie with B-221 to 
top of basalt is not real good in this area, it does show bedrock to be considerable shallower from 
the depths obtained approximately 2,200-2,300 feet west of this location.   
Conclusions 
The acquisition of 2D seismic data along a portion of the HHCTCP alignment provided very 
good imaging of the overburden soils and the soil/bedrock contact.  The ReMi method was 
selected because of its ability to acquire seismic data in (very) noisy urban environments and to 
distinguish between soft soils, stiffer soils and bedrock based on their shear-wave velocity.  The 
ReMi passive surface-wave method is not affected by the presence of a shallow water table, 
which was important as much of the survey area was very near the coast.   
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc. 
12 
Final Report 
This geophysical survey met the primary project objective - delineating areas of soft soils. This 
is corroborated by geotechnical boring data. Correlation with geologic logs for thick soft soil 
zones and depth to bedrock was very good.  The ReMi data indicate areas of thick (>60 feet) soft 
soils, interpreted to be less than 600 ft/s materials.  Soils with a shear-wave velocity of less than 
600 ft/s are classified as Class E (soft soils) according to the 2006 International Building Code.  
Soils with shear-wave velocities between 600 ft/s and 1,200 ft/s are classified as Class D (stiff 
soils). 
There was general agreement between the borehole data and geophysical data to reasonably state 
the secondary survey objective - image the soil/bedrock contact - was achieved.  Based on boring 
logs from the holes along ReMi Lines 1 through 5, bedrock conditions are not expected to be 
uniform or homogenous within the survey area.  As such, some variability of the shear-wave 
velocity is expected to exist in the transition zone from stiff soils to weathered bedrock to 
competent bedrock.  The choice of a 2,000 ft/s shear-wave velocity contour, shown on each 
cross-section, was used to depict the interpreted bedrock velocity.  It was based on the upper 
range Vs values for stiff soils transitioning to soft rock. ReMi results collected adjacent to 
geotechnical borings indicate that the soil/basalt contact depths correlate very well with modeled 
shear-wave velocities between 1,800 ft/s and 2,400 ft/s.  Shear wave velocities of 1,800 ft/s in a 
rock mass would generally indicate intensely weathered or highly fractured rock; and, shear 
wave velocities of 2,400 ft/s or greater generally indicates competent bedrock.  Materials with 
shear-wave velocities between 1,200 and 2,500 ft/s are classified as Class C (IBC 2006) – very 
dense soil or soft rock.  Materials with shear-wave velocities greater than 2,500 ft/s are classified 
as Class B - rock.  The 2,000 ft/s contour line used to depict the ‘normalized’ bedrock surface is 
likely not appropriate from the western portion of the survey area (East Kapolei) to the east end 
of the survey area (Pearl Highlands). 
Acknowledgements 
Zonge appreciates the opportunity to perform the 2D refraction microtremor survey along this 
portion of the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project.  We wish to express sincere 
appreciation to Geolabs personnel, as well as the subcontractors that supported the field work.  
Without their continued effort and support this geophysical investigation would not have 
succeeded. We believe the seismic investigation successfully met both objectives and showed 
that 2D ReMi profiling can be used in the busy urban/highway setting with highly variable 
subsurface soils and changing depth-to-bedrock.  If you have any questions regarding the seismic 
data acquisition, processing, or interpretation, please do not hesitate to contact us. 
GDR 03/27/2009
Project #08126 Geolabs, Inc.

APPENDIX A 
METHOD BRIEF: 
AN INTRODUCTION TO 
THE REFRACTION MICROTREMOR 
SURVEY METHOD 
GDR 03/27/2009
Zonge Geosciences, Inc. 
1990 S. Garrison Street, Suite #2 
Lakewood, CO  80227 
Phone: (720) 962-4444 
INTRODUCTION TO THE REFRACTION MICROTREMOR METHOD – ReMiTM 
OBJECTIVE: 
The objective of the geophysical survey is to determine in-situ shear-wave (S-wave) 
velocities of the overburden soil deposits and the bedrock (if encountered) to arrive at a 
“Vs30”; that is, the average shear-wave velocity to 30 meters (100 feet).  The Vs data 
are to be acquired for site classification purposes as prescribed in Section 1615.1.1 of 
IBC 2000. The Refraction Microtremor (ReMi) method does not require the use of a 
borehole or active seismic sources (impact hammers, blasting, etc.). 
FIELD METHODS: 
To meet the objective, Zonge GeoSciences (Zonge) recommends conducting a set of 
passive surface-wave seismic tests at the project site, using the new technology of 
Refraction Microtremor – ReMi.  The proposed work is accomplished in noisy urban 
environments using a specialized array of surface geophones (low frequency phones) 
and measuring the ambient seismic vibrations.  The field setup involves laying out a 100-
meter line with 12 geophones spaced 8m apart (~300 ft and ~26 ft, respectively).  A 
seismograph is set to record data over a 30 second time interval, which is repeated 
several times (as many as 10) for signal processing purposes.  These data are 
processed to develop a Vs versus depth curve to 30 meters (100 feet).  
The technology for ReMi is based on the useful Spectral Analysis of Surface (SASW), 
and more recently the Multi-channel Analysis of Surface Waves (MASW) methods.  
Surface waves comprise about 80% of the energy from a hammer blows, weight drops 
or surface vibrations caused by ambient energy (e.g., vehicles, construction, drilling and 
sampling, etc.).  The picture below illustrates the field setup for MASW.  SASW uses 
only 4 receivers, ReMi uses 12 receivers, but they both produce a 1D shear wave 
velocity (Vs) curve.  Low velocity layers at depth can be detected with either the SASW, 
MASW, or ReMi techniques.  The only significant difference between SASW or MASW 
and ReMi seismic testing is that no active sources are required for ReMi – it monitors the 
‘passive’ energy in the ground, or microtremors. 
GDR 03/27/2009
Zonge Geosciences, Inc. 
1990 S. Garrison Street, Suite #2 
Lakewood, CO  80227 
Phone: (720) 962-4444 
Using ReMi in a similar fashion, and combining the data acquisition procedures with 
some MASW-style measurements (or active energy sources), provides a profile of Vs.  
For projects where Vs30 is the only requirement, only one array set up is required for 
ReMi.  However, it is recommended by most geotechnical engineers that multiple set-
ups be placed around the proposed structure(s) to determine if lateral variability of the 
soil thickness or stiffness must be taken into account for the design.   
The ReMi method has proved to be extremely useful in urban settings where standard 
surface seismic methods fail, and borehole methods (e.g., downhole or crosshole 
seismic) can have data that are difficult to 
interpret in the upper 10 meters (20-30 
feet). The picture to the left shows the 
ReMi system deployed along 14th Street in 
downtown Denver, Colorado.  A shear-
wave profile is presented from the ReMi 
field measurements, and a table of 
velocities for the upper 30 meters.  The 
Excel sheet containing the velocities 
automatically computes the average over 
the upper 30 meters (Vs30).  Depending 
on the site conditions and ambient noise 
sources nearby, this ReMi test method 
can determine Vs to much greater depths, 
as evidenced in Reno by Dr. J. Louie (2001), from the University of Nevada-Reno.  The 
ReMi approach for Vs30 has been developed by Optim, a adjunct professional 
organization to the University at Reno (Mackay School of Mines).  The ReMi software 
uses an algorithm to compute the dispersion curve form the ambient noise 
measurements and forward model the data to a Vs curve.  ReMi Vs measurements have 
been consistently shown to be within 10% or less of standard downhole or crosshole 
measurements.  See example below (Bureau of Reclamation 2002).  Additionally, Zonge 
has collected ReMi and downhole seismic on the same site and shown less than 10 
percent difference in layer thickness, shear-wave velocity, and computed Vs30. 
Zonge Geosciences has completed over 50 ReMi surveys in the front-range corridor of 
Colorado in the past 2 ½ years.  The method has been effective for determining Vs30 at 
quite sites (where we need to impart energy into the ground), at construction sites, and 
in suburban/residential settings.   
Example data plots and results table are attached below for reference.

APPENDIX E 
Field Point Load Testing 
GDR 03/27/2009
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page E-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   E 
Field Point Load Testing 
Field point load testing was performed on selected rock cores obtained during 
our field exploration program in general accordance with ASTM D 5731 – Determination 
of the Point Load Strength Index of Rock and Application to Rock Strength 
Classifications.   
The point load test measures the Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) of rock 
specimens in the form of either core (the diametral and axial tests), cut blocks (the block 
test), or irregular lumps (the irregular lump test). The rock specimens are broken by 
application of concentrated load through a pair of spherically truncated, conical platens.  
For this project, Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) were determined from two hundred 
sixty-seven (267) diametral and axial tests performed on selected rock cores retrieved 
at B-105, B-107, B-108, B-112, B-209 through B-211, B-213 through B-218, B-221, 
B-224, B-226, B-303, and B-304 during our field exploration program. Tests with 
fracture surface passing through only one loading point were rejected and not reported 
in this appendix. 
The field point load tests were performed using a Model 77-0110 Point Load Test 
Apparatus manufactured by ELE International and provided by PB Americas, Inc. The 
apparatus consists of a rigid frame, two point load platens, a hydraulically activated ram 
with pressure gauge and a device for measuring the distance between the loading 
points. The pressure gauge is of the type in which the failure pressure can be recorded. 
The test involves compressing a rock sample between conical steel plates until failure 
occurs. The following data were recorded from which the Uncorrected Point Load Strength 
Index (Is) of the specimen was determined: 
Is  = P / De
2, MPa 
where: 
P = failure load, N, 
De = equivalent core diameter, mm, and is given by: 
De
2  = D2 for diametral core tests without penetration, mm2 
or 
De
2  = 4A/π for axial, block, and lump tests, mm2 
where: 
A = WD = minimum cross-sectional area of a plane through the platen contact 
points  
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix E 
Field Point Load Testing 
W.O. 6000-10   
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page E-2 
Hawaii ● California 
The Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) then is obtained by applying a 
“Size Correction Factor F” to the Uncorrected Point Load Strength Index (Is): 
Is(50) = F * Is  
where: 
F = (De/50)0.45 
The estimated uniaxial compressive strength (σc) may be obtained by the 
following formula: 
σc = K * Is(D) 
where: 
σc  = uniaxial compressive strength, MPa 
K    = index to strength conversion factor that depends on site-specific correlation 
between σc and Is(D), MPa 
Is(D) = corrected point load strength index at a given (D) 
The field data collected and the determination of the Point Load Strength Index 
(Is(50)) and the estimated uniaxial compressive strength (σc) are presented on Plates E-1 
through E-6. 
(h:\6000Series\6000-10.tk3 – p48)

A P P E N D I X   F 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled 
for the project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and 
summarized in Table F-1.  In addition, groundwater monitoring points were established at 
four boring locations, including B-110(P), B-217(P), B-219(P), and B-221(P), to monitor 
seasonal fluctuations and to measure groundwater gradients along the project segment 
under consideration in this report. The boring locations where these groundwater 
monitoring points are established are shown in the following table and on the 
Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.4. 
GROUNDWATER MONITORING POINTS

+17.6 
A total of 10 vibrating wire piezometers, Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon, 
Inc., were installed at selected depths within the borings to monitor the groundwater 
levels on a near-continuous basis using a Model LC-2 datalogger manufactured by 
Geokon, Inc. Catalog sheets of the vibrating wire piezometer and datalogger are 
presented in Figures F-1 and F-2 for illustration purposes only. The actual elevations of 
the vibrating wire piezometers are reflected on the installation details prepared for each 
monitoring point as shown on Plates F-1.1, F2.1, F-3.1 and F-4.1. 
Prior to the installation, the filter stone is saturated by placing the piezometer under 
water a minimum of 12 hours and transported to the field in a submerged condition. In 
addition, zero readings were obtained in the field prior to the installation of each 
piezometer, which provided a baseline reference under field conditions and served as an 
acceptance test – a verification that the piezometer is working properly. The piezometers 
are embedded in a filter sand pack and sealed at the top and bottom with bentonite to 
isolate the piezometer at the specific strata where groundwater level measurements are 
desired. 
The groundwater level data were downloaded in the field to a laptop computer on 
a regular basis and processed in our office. It should be noted that there are some 
periods where the data acquisition lost some of the data collected resulting in blanks in 
the data set. However, it does not appear that there are significant anomalies in the

groundwater levels to raise concerns based on the other available groundwater level 
monitoring data. Typical groundwater level data obtained by the piezometers are shown 
on Table F-2. Graphic presentations of the groundwater level data collected are provided 
on Plates F-1.2 through F-4.4.

GDR 03/27/2009
VIBRATING WIRE PIEZOMETERS 
Standard Piezometers 
Model 4500S(H), 4500AL(V) 
The Model 4500 Standard Piezometer is designed to measure fluid
pressures such as ground water elevations and pore pressures when
buried directly in embankments, fills, etc. Also for installation inside
boreholes, observation wells and standard (>19 mm diameter)
piezometer riser pipe. The Model 4500SH is designed with a heavy
duty housing for pressures that exceed 3 MPa. The Model 4500AL is
designed for low-pressure ranges. The vented version (ALV) provides
automatic 
compensation 
for 
barometric 
pressure 
changes.
Thermistors are included to measure temperatures.* 
Specifications 
4500S 
4500SH 
4500AL(V) 
Standard Ranges 
350, 700 kPa; 
1, 2, 3 MPa 
5, 7.5 MPa 
70, 170 kPa 
Over Range

GDR 03/27/2009
SINGLE-CHANNEL DATALOGGER 
Single-Channel Dataloggers 
Model 8002-1, 8002-1A (LC-2, LC-2A) 
The Model 8002-1 (LC-2) and Model 8002-1A (LC-2A) are
stand-alone, single-channel dataloggers, designed to read both the
vibrating wire element and the integral 
thermistor 
of any
Geokon vibrating wire sensor. The LC-2 offers an internal hard wired
transducer connection and the LC-2A provides an optional 10-pin
transducer connector for rapid sensor connection. The dataloggers
are housed inside a Fiberglass Nema 4X enclosure, are very robust,
weather-proof and particularly well-suited to operation in harsh
environments. Power is supplied by two easily accessible ‘D’ cells or
by solar panel for remote long-term applications. 16,000 arrays can
be stored (8,000 arrays when used with LogWare) before the oldest
data is over-written. Strings of LC-2 dataloggers can be daisy
chained using the RS-485 interface, and versions are available with
radios and modems to provide remote readout capabilities. Complete
with Windows® based, user-friendly software.

Resolution

APPENDIX G 
Laboratory Testing 
GDR 03/27/2009
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page G-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   G 
Laboratory Testing 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the segment 
extending from East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands of the HHCTCP were visually examined 
and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii.  Selected samples were tested to 
evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties of the soils 
encountered. The laboratory testing program was formulated with the emphasis on 
evaluating the moisture, density, plasticity, gradation, compressibility, shear strength, 
and corrosivity of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture 
content, 
Atterberg 
Limits, 
sieve 
analysis, 
hydrometer, 
unconfined 
compression, triaxial compression, direct shear, consolidation, Modified Proctor 
compaction, and California Bearing Ratio (CBR), corrosion, and expansion tests.  
Results of the tests performed are summarized in Tables G-1.1 through G-3 and are 
presented on Plates G-1 through G-180.  Detailed descriptions of the various tests 
performed for this exploration are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture content and dry density tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to aid in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and 
correlating the layers between test borings.  The moisture content tests were performed 
in general accordance with ASTM Test Designation D 2216.  The test results are 
summarized in Tables G-1.1 through G-1.31 and are presented on the Logs of Borings 
at the appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
One hundred and thirty-one (131) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on 
selected soil samples to evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity 
index (PI), as an aid in classifying the soils.  The Atterberg Limits tests were performed 
in general accordance with ASTM D 4318.  The test results are summarized on the 
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths and in Tables G-1.1 through G-1.31.  
Graphic presentation of the test results are provided on Plates G-1 through G-15. 
Sieve Analysis and Hydrometer 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
forty-nine (49) Sieve Analysis tests including one (1) hydrometer test were performed to 
evaluate the gradation characteristics and to aid in soil classification. The tests were 
performed in accordance with ASTM C 136 and D 422.  Graphic presentation of the test 
results are provided on Plates G-16 through G-26.  In addition, twenty-one (21) tests 
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix G 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page G-2 
Hawaii ● California 
were conducted on selected soil samples and core runs to evaluate the amount of fine 
content.  The tests were performed in accordance with ASTM D 1140.  The test results 
are summarized on Plate G-27. 
Unconfined Compression – Soil Samples 
To evaluate the unconfined compressive strength of the on-site clayey soils, 
twenty-five (25) Unconfined Compression tests were performed on selected in-situ 
samples in accordance with ASTM D 2166.  The test results are shown on the Logs of 
Borings at the appropriate sample depths and summarized on Table G-2. Individual 
stress-strain curves of the unconfined compression tests are presented on Plates G-28 
through G-52. 
Unconfined Compression – Rock Cores 
Forty-four (44) Unconfined Compression tests (ASTM D 7012 Method C) were 
performed on selected rock cores to evaluate their unconfined compressive strength of 
the rock formation encountered.  Unconfined compression test results are presented on 
Plates G-53 through G-55. 
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, sixty (60) 
unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 2850.  The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” 
samples.  The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates G-56 
through G-115. 
Consolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the soft clayey soils, four (4) 
Consolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected in-situ 
soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 4767.  The test results and the stress-strain 
curves at different confining pressures are presented on Plates G-116 through G-119. 
Direct Shear 
To evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the in-situ soils, thirty-seven 
(37) strained-controlled, consolidated-drained Direct Shear tests were performed on the 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 3080.  The test results are presented 
on Plates G-120 through G-156. 
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix G 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page G-3 
Hawaii ● California 
Consolidation 
Eight Consolidation tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate 
the compressibility characteristics of the on-site soils.  These tests were performed in 
accordance with ASTM D 2435, and the test results are plotted on Plates G-157 through 
G-164. 
Modified Proctor Test 
Five laboratory compaction tests were performed on the bulk samples obtained 
to evaluate the relationship between the moisture content and the dry density of the 
near-surface soils. The Modified Proctor tests were performed in accordance with 
ASTM D 1557 using the moist preparation method (wet to dry).  The test results are 
summarized in Table G-3 and are graphically presented on Plates G-165 through 
G-169. 
California Bearing Ratio (CBR)  
Five CBR tests were performed on the near-surface soils to evaluate the strength 
of the in-situ materials for pavement support.  The tests were performed in accordance 
with ASTM D 1883 near the natural moisture content. The test results are summarized 
in Table G-3 and are presented on Plates G-170 through G-174. 
Corrosion Test 
Thirty-seven (37) sets of pH (ASTM G51) and minimum resistivity (ASTM G57) 
tests were performed on selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration by 
our office. In addition, twenty-nine (29) sets of chloride (EPA 300) and sulfate (EPA 300) 
were performed by TestAmerica, Inc. on selected soil samples obtained from our field 
exploration.  The test results are presented on Plates G-175 and G-176. 
Ring Swell Test 
Nine (9) one-inch Ring Swell tests were performed on relatively undisturbed 
samples to evaluate the swelling potential of the near-surface soils under a small 
surcharge pressure.  The test results are summarized on Plates G-177 and G-178. 
Expansion Index of Soil Test 
Four (4) Expansion Index of Soil tests were performed on the bulk samples of the 
near-surface soils at selected locations in general accordance with ASTM D 4829.  The 
test is a standardized test used to determine the expansive characteristics of the soil.  
The Expansion Index of Soil test is the same test (UBC Standard 18-2) described in the 
Uniform Building Code (UBC) to determine the expansive characteristics of soils.  The 
test results are summarized on Plate G-179. 
GDR 03/27/2009
Appendix G 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page G-4 
Hawaii ● California 
Organic Content 
Four (4) organic content tests (ASTM D 2974) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the amount of organic matter.  The test results are presented on 
Plate G-180.

APPENDIX I 
Selected Data from 1991 Geotechnical Engineering Exploration 
GDR 03/27/2009
W.O. 6000-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MARCH 2009     Page I-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   I 
Selected Data from 1991 Geotechnical Engineering Exploration 
A geotechnical engineering exploration was conducted by Geolabs-Hawaii in 
1991 for the Honolulu Rapid Transit Development Project. Selected boring logs and 
laboratory test data from the 1991 study that may be applicable to this project are 
included in this appendix for information and reference purposes. The selected 
subsurface information included four borings, designated as B-1 through B-4, at the 
locations shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 3.4. Please note the boring locations 
for these four borings were not surveyed. 
(h:\6000Series\6000-10.tk3 – p69)
